<p><b>Siemens <a href="/wiki/Aktiengesellschaft" title="Aktiengesellschaft">AG</a></b> (<small>German pronunciation:</small> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_German" title="Help:IPA for German">[ˈziːmɛns]</a></span>)<sup id="cite_ref-pronouncekiwi_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pronouncekiwi-2">[2]</a></sup> is a German conglomerate company headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> and the largest manufacturing and electronics company in Europe with branch offices abroad.</p>
<p>The principal divisions of the company are <i>Industry</i>, <i>Energy</i>, <i>Healthcare</i>, and <i>Infrastructure &amp; Cities</i>, which represent the main activities of the company.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The company is a prominent maker of medical diagnostics equipment and its medical health-care division, which generates about 12 percent of the company's total sales, is its second-most profitable unit, after the industrial automation division.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The company is a component of the <a href="/wiki/Euro_Stoxx_50" title="Euro Stoxx 50">Euro Stoxx 50</a> <a href="/wiki/Stock_market_index" title="Stock market index">stock market index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Siemens and its subsidiaries employ approximately 362,000 people worldwide and reported global revenue of around €75.6 billion in 2015 according to their annual report. The von Siemens family remain the largest shareholder with a stake of 6.9% percent.</p>
<p></p>

</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1847_to_1901">1847 to 1901</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1847 to 1901">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Werner-von-Siemens.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Werner-von-Siemens.png/160px-Werner-von-Siemens.png" width="160" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Werner-von-Siemens.png/240px-Werner-von-Siemens.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Werner-von-Siemens.png/320px-Werner-von-Siemens.png 2x" data-file-width="1494" data-file-height="2055" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Werner-von-Siemens.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Werner_von_Siemens" title="Werner von Siemens">Werner von Siemens</a>, co-founder of <a href="/wiki/Siemens_%26_Halske" title="Siemens &amp; Halske">Siemens &amp; Halske</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Siemens_%26_Halske" title="Siemens &amp; Halske">Siemens &amp; Halske</a> was founded by <a href="/wiki/Werner_von_Siemens" title="Werner von Siemens">Werner von Siemens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_Halske" title="Johann Georg Halske">Johann Georg Halske</a> on 12 October 1847. Based on the <a href="/wiki/Telegraph" class="mw-redirect" title="Telegraph">telegraph</a>, his invention used a needle to point to the sequence of letters, instead of using <a href="/wiki/Morse_code" title="Morse code">Morse code</a>. The company, then called <i>Telegraphen-Bauanstalt von Siemens &amp; Halske</i>, opened its first workshop on 12 October.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1848, the company built the first long-distance telegraph line in Europe; 500&#160;km from Berlin to <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_am_Main" class="mw-redirect" title="Frankfurt am Main">Frankfurt am Main</a>. In 1850, the founder's younger brother, Carl Wilhelm Siemens, later <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Siemens" title="Carl Wilhelm Siemens">Sir William Siemens</a>, started to represent the company in London. The <a href="/wiki/Siemens_Brothers" title="Siemens Brothers">London agency</a> became a branch office in 1858. In the 1850s, the company was involved in building long distance telegraph networks in Russia. In 1855, a company branch headed by another brother, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Heinrich_von_Siemens" title="Carl Heinrich von Siemens">Carl Heinrich von Siemens</a>, opened in <a href="/wiki/St_Petersburg" class="mw-redirect" title="St Petersburg">St Petersburg</a>, Russia. In 1867, Siemens completed the monumental Indo-European (<a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Calcutta</a> to <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>) telegraph line.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PEZ.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/PEZ.jpg/230px-PEZ.jpg" width="230" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/PEZ.jpg/345px-PEZ.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/PEZ.jpg/460px-PEZ.jpg 2x" data-file-width="778" data-file-height="488" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PEZ.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First <a href="/wiki/Electric_locomotive" title="Electric locomotive">electric locomotive</a>, built in 1879 by company founder Werner von Siemens.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1867, Werner von Siemens described a <a href="/wiki/Dynamo" title="Dynamo">dynamo</a> without permanent magnets. A similar system was also independently invented by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wheatstone" title="Charles Wheatstone">Charles Wheatstone</a>, but Siemens became the first company to build such devices. In 1881, a Siemens <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">AC</a> <a href="/wiki/Alternator" title="Alternator">Alternator</a> driven by a <a href="/wiki/Watermill" title="Watermill">watermill</a> was used to power the world's first electric street lighting in the town of <a href="/wiki/Godalming" title="Godalming">Godalming</a>, United Kingdom. The company continued to grow and diversified into <a href="/wiki/Electric_trains" class="mw-redirect" title="Electric trains">electric trains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Light_bulbs" class="mw-redirect" title="Light bulbs">light bulbs</a>. In 1890, the founder retired and left running the company to his brother Carl and sons Arnold and Wilhelm. In 1887, it opened its first office in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1901_to_1933">1901 to 1933</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1901 to 1933">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1985-0607-500,_Ernst_Udet_in_Flugzeug_%22Flamingo%22.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1985-0607-500%2C_Ernst_Udet_in_Flugzeug_%22Flamingo%22.jpg/230px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1985-0607-500%2C_Ernst_Udet_in_Flugzeug_%22Flamingo%22.jpg" width="230" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1985-0607-500%2C_Ernst_Udet_in_Flugzeug_%22Flamingo%22.jpg/345px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1985-0607-500%2C_Ernst_Udet_in_Flugzeug_%22Flamingo%22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1985-0607-500%2C_Ernst_Udet_in_Flugzeug_%22Flamingo%22.jpg/460px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1985-0607-500%2C_Ernst_Udet_in_Flugzeug_%22Flamingo%22.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="551" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1985-0607-500,_Ernst_Udet_in_Flugzeug_%22Flamingo%22.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The company built airplanes during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, for example this Siemens airplane in 1926.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Siemens &amp; Halske (S &amp; H) was incorporated in 1897, and then merged parts of its activities with Schuckert &amp; Co., Nuremberg in 1903 to become <a href="/wiki/Siemens-Schuckert" title="Siemens-Schuckert">Siemens-Schuckert</a>. In 1907, Siemens (<a href="/wiki/Siemens_%26_Halske" title="Siemens &amp; Halske">Siemens &amp; Halske</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siemens-Schuckert" title="Siemens-Schuckert">Siemens-Schuckert</a>) had 34,324 employees and was the seventh-largest company in the German empire by number of employees.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> (see <a href="/wiki/List_of_German_companies_by_employees_in_1907" title="List of German companies by employees in 1907">List of German companies by employees in 1907</a>)</p>
<p>In 1919, S &amp; H and two other companies jointly formed the <a href="/wiki/Osram" title="Osram">Osram</a> <a href="/wiki/Lightbulb" class="mw-redirect" title="Lightbulb">lightbulb</a> company.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>During the 1920s and 1930s, S &amp; H started to manufacture <a href="/wiki/Radios" class="mw-redirect" title="Radios">radios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Television_set" title="Television set">television sets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Electron_microscopes" class="mw-redirect" title="Electron microscopes">electron microscopes</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1932, <a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reiniger,_Gebbert_%26_Schall" class="extiw" title="de:Reiniger, Gebbert &amp; Schall">Reiniger, Gebbert &amp; Schall</a> (Erlangen), <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ph%C3%B6nix_AG&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Phönix AG (page does not exist)">Phönix AG</a> (Rudolstadt) and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens-Reiniger-Veifa_mbH&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Siemens-Reiniger-Veifa mbH (page does not exist)">Siemens-Reiniger-Veifa mbH</a> (Berlin) merged to form the <a href="/wiki/Siemens-Reiniger-Werke_AG" class="mw-redirect" title="Siemens-Reiniger-Werke AG">Siemens-Reiniger-Werke AG</a> (SRW), the third of the so-called parent companies that merged in 1966 to form the present-day Siemens AG.<sup id="cite_ref-historysite_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historysite-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1920s, Siemens constructed the <a href="/wiki/Ardnacrusha_power_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Ardnacrusha power plant">Ardnacrusha</a> Hydro Power station on the <a href="/wiki/River_Shannon" title="River Shannon">River Shannon</a> in the then <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>, and it was a world first for its design. The company is remembered for its desire to raise the wages of its under-paid workers only to be overruled by the <a href="/wiki/Cumann_na_nGaedheal" title="Cumann na nGaedheal">Cumann na nGaedheal</a> government.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1933_to_1945">1933 to 1945</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1933 to 1945">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USHMM95273_-Siemens_factory_in_KZ_Bobrek.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/USHMM95273_-Siemens_factory_in_KZ_Bobrek.jpg/230px-USHMM95273_-Siemens_factory_in_KZ_Bobrek.jpg" width="230" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/USHMM95273_-Siemens_factory_in_KZ_Bobrek.jpg/345px-USHMM95273_-Siemens_factory_in_KZ_Bobrek.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/USHMM95273_-Siemens_factory_in_KZ_Bobrek.jpg/460px-USHMM95273_-Siemens_factory_in_KZ_Bobrek.jpg 2x" data-file-width="636" data-file-height="450" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USHMM95273_-Siemens_factory_in_KZ_Bobrek.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Prisoners around 1944 working at a Siemens factory in KZ Bobrek, a subcamp of <a href="/wiki/Auschwitz_concentration_camp" title="Auschwitz concentration camp">Auschwitz concentration camp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iechmnnjersulame_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iechmnnjersulame-14">[14]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R97782,_Berlin,_Wahlwerbung_der_NSDAP_in.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R97782%2C_Berlin%2C_Wahlwerbung_der_NSDAP_in.jpg/230px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R97782%2C_Berlin%2C_Wahlwerbung_der_NSDAP_in.jpg" width="230" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R97782%2C_Berlin%2C_Wahlwerbung_der_NSDAP_in.jpg/345px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R97782%2C_Berlin%2C_Wahlwerbung_der_NSDAP_in.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R97782%2C_Berlin%2C_Wahlwerbung_der_NSDAP_in.jpg/460px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R97782%2C_Berlin%2C_Wahlwerbung_der_NSDAP_in.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="544" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R97782,_Berlin,_Wahlwerbung_der_NSDAP_in.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Siemens truck being used as a <a href="/wiki/Nazi" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazi">Nazi</a> <a href="/wiki/Public_address" class="mw-redirect" title="Public address">public address</a> vehicle in 1932</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Siemens (at the time: <a href="/wiki/Siemens-Schuckert" title="Siemens-Schuckert">Siemens-Schuckert</a>) exploited the forced labour of deported people in <a href="/wiki/Extermination_camps" class="mw-redirect" title="Extermination camps">extermination camps</a>. The company owned a plant in <a href="/wiki/Auschwitz_concentration_camp" title="Auschwitz concentration camp">Auschwitz concentration camp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iechmnnjersulame_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iechmnnjersulame-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the final years of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, numerous plants and factories in Berlin and other major cities were destroyed by Allied air raids. To prevent further losses, manufacturing was therefore moved to alternative places and regions not affected by the air war. The goal was to secure continued production of important war-related and everyday goods. According to records, Siemens was operating almost 400 alternative or relocated manufacturing plants at the end of 1944 and in early 1945.</p>
<p>In 1972, Siemens sued German satirist F.C. Delius for his satirical history of the company, <i>Unsere Siemenswelt</i>, and it was determined much of the book contained false claims although the trial itself publicized Siemens' history in Nazi Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The company supplied electrical parts to <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">Nazi concentration camps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Death_camps" class="mw-redirect" title="Death camps">death camps</a>. The factories had poor working conditions, where malnutrition and death were common. Also, the scholarship has shown that the camp factories were created, run, and supplied by the <a href="/wiki/SS" class="mw-redirect" title="SS">SS</a>, in conjunction with company officials, sometimes high-level officials.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Siemens businessman and <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a> member <a href="/wiki/John_Rabe" title="John Rabe">John Rabe</a> is, however, credited with saving many Chinese lives during the infamous <a href="/wiki/Nanking_Massacre" title="Nanking Massacre">Nanking Massacre</a>. He later toured Germany lecturing on the atrocities committed by Japanese forces in Nanking.<sup id="cite_ref-John_Rabe_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_Rabe-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1945_to_2001">1945 to 2001</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1945 to 2001">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 1950s, and from their new base in <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a>, S&amp;H started to manufacture <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_device" title="Semiconductor device">semiconductor devices</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washing_machines" class="mw-redirect" title="Washing machines">washing machines</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Artificial_pacemaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial pacemaker">pacemakers</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 1966, <a href="/wiki/Siemens_%26_Halske" title="Siemens &amp; Halske">Siemens &amp; Halske</a> (S&amp;H, founded in 1847), <a href="/wiki/Siemens-Schuckert" title="Siemens-Schuckert">Siemens-Schuckertwerke</a> (SSW, founded in 1903) and <a href="/wiki/Siemens-Reiniger-Werke" class="mw-redirect" title="Siemens-Reiniger-Werke">Siemens-Reiniger-Werke</a> (SRW, founded in 1932) merged to form Siemens AG.<sup id="cite_ref-historysite_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historysite-12">[12]</a></sup> In 1969, Siemens formed Kraftwerk Union with <a href="/wiki/AEG" title="AEG">AEG</a> by pooling their nuclear power businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ110415_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ110415-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg/160px-Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg" width="160" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg/240px-Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg/320px-Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg 2x" data-file-width="454" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 1973 Siemens <a href="/wiki/Electron_microscope" title="Electron microscope">electron microscope</a> on display at the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_des_Arts_et_M%C3%A9tiers" title="Musée des Arts et Métiers">Musée des Arts et Métiers</a> in Paris.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The company's first digital telephone exchange was produced in 1980. In 1988, Siemens and <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_Company_plc" title="General Electric Company plc">GEC</a> acquired the UK defence and technology company <a href="/wiki/Plessey" title="Plessey">Plessey</a>. Plessey's holdings were split, and Siemens took over the <a href="/wiki/Avionics" title="Avionics">avionics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> and traffic control businesses—as <a href="/wiki/Siemens_Plessey" title="Siemens Plessey">Siemens Plessey</a>.</p>
<p>In 1985, Siemens bought <a href="/wiki/Allis-Chalmers_Manufacturing_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Allis-Chalmers Manufacturing Company">Allis-Chalmers</a>' interest in the partnership company <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens-Allis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Siemens-Allis (page does not exist)">Siemens-Allis</a> (formed 1978) which supplied electrical control equipment. It was incorporated into Siemens' Energy and Automation division.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1987, Siemens reintegrated Kraftwerk Union, the unit overseeing nuclear power business.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ110415_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ110415-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1991, Siemens acquired <a href="/wiki/Nixdorf_Computer_AG" class="mw-redirect" title="Nixdorf Computer AG">Nixdorf Computer AG</a> and renamed it <a href="/wiki/Siemens_Nixdorf_Informationssysteme" title="Siemens Nixdorf Informationssysteme">Siemens Nixdorf Informationssysteme</a> AG, in order to produce <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computers</a>.</p>
<p>In October 1991, Siemens acquired the Industrial Systems Division of <a href="/wiki/Texas_Instruments" title="Texas Instruments">Texas Instruments</a>, Inc, based in <a href="/wiki/Johnson_City,_Tennessee" title="Johnson City, Tennessee">Johnson City, Tennessee</a>. This division was organized as Siemens Industrial Automation, Inc., and was later absorbed by Siemens Energy and Automation, Inc.</p>
<p>In 1992, Siemens bought out <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>'s half of <a href="/wiki/ROLM" title="ROLM">ROLM</a> (Siemens had bought into ROLM five years earlier), thus creating SiemensROLM Communications; eventually dropping ROLM from the name later in the 1990s.</p>
<p>In 1997, Siemens agreed to sell the defence arm of <a href="/wiki/Siemens_Plessey" title="Siemens Plessey">Siemens Plessey</a> to <a href="/wiki/British_Aerospace" title="British Aerospace">British Aerospace</a> (BAe) and a German aerospace company, <a href="/wiki/DaimlerChrysler_Aerospace" class="mw-redirect" title="DaimlerChrysler Aerospace">DaimlerChrysler Aerospace</a>. BAe and DASA acquired the British and German divisions of the operation respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 1997, Siemens Financial Services (SFS) was founded to act as competence center for financing issues and as a manager of financial risks within Siemens.</p>
<p>In 1998, Siemens acquired <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_Company" title="Westinghouse Electric Company">Westinghouse Power Generation</a> for more than $1.5 billion from CBS corp and moving Siemens from third to second the world power generation market.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1999, Siemens' semiconductor operations were spun off into a new company called <a href="/wiki/Infineon_Technologies" title="Infineon Technologies">Infineon Technologies</a>. In the same year, Siemens Nixdorf Informationssysteme AG became part of <a href="/wiki/Fujitsu_Siemens_Computers" title="Fujitsu Siemens Computers">Fujitsu Siemens Computers</a> AG, with its <a href="/wiki/Retail_banking" title="Retail banking">retail banking</a> technology group becoming <a href="/wiki/Wincor_Nixdorf" title="Wincor Nixdorf">Wincor Nixdorf</a>.</p>
<p>In 2000, Shared Medical Systems Corporation<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> was acquired by the Siemens' Medical Engineering Group,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> eventually becoming part of <a href="/wiki/Siemens_Medical_Solutions" class="mw-redirect" title="Siemens Medical Solutions">Siemens Medical Solutions</a>.</p>
<p>Also in 2000, Atecs-Mannesman was acquired by Siemens,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The sale was finalised in April 2001 with 50% of the shares acquired, acquisition, <i>Mannesmann VDO AG</i> merged into Siemens Automotive forming Siemens VDO Automotive AG, <i>Atecs Mannesmann Dematic Systems</i> merged into Siemens Production and Logistics forming Siemens Dematic AG, <i>Mannesmann Demag Delaval</i> merged into the Power Generation division of Siemens AG.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Other parts of the company were acquired by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bosch_GmbH" title="Robert Bosch GmbH">Robert Bosch GmbH</a> at the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Also, Moore Products Co. of Spring House, PA USA was acquired by Siemens Energy &amp; Automation, Inc.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2001_to_2011">2001 to 2011</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2001 to 2011">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ICE_3_Oberhaider-Wald-Tunnel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/ICE_3_Oberhaider-Wald-Tunnel.jpg/220px-ICE_3_Oberhaider-Wald-Tunnel.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/ICE_3_Oberhaider-Wald-Tunnel.jpg/330px-ICE_3_Oberhaider-Wald-Tunnel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/ICE_3_Oberhaider-Wald-Tunnel.jpg/440px-ICE_3_Oberhaider-Wald-Tunnel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ICE_3_Oberhaider-Wald-Tunnel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Siemens_Velaro" title="Siemens Velaro">Siemens Velaro</a> high speed train in service on the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ln%E2%80%93Frankfurt_high-speed_rail_line" title="Köln–Frankfurt high-speed rail line">Köln–Frankfurt high-speed rail line</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2001, Chemtech Group of Brazil was incorporated into the Siemens Group;<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> it provides industrial process optimisation, consultancy and other engineering services.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also in 2001, Siemens formed joint venture Framatome with <a href="/wiki/Areva" title="Areva">Areva SA</a> of France by merging much of the companies' nuclear businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ110415_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ110415-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2002, Siemens sold some of its business activities to <a href="/wiki/Kohlberg_Kravis_Roberts_%26_Co." class="mw-redirect" title="Kohlberg Kravis Roberts &amp; Co.">Kohlberg Kravis Roberts &amp; Co.</a> L.P. (KKR), with its metering business included in the sale package.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2003, Siemens acquired the flow division of <a href="/wiki/Danfoss" title="Danfoss">Danfoss</a> and incorporated it into the Automation and Drives division.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Also in 2003 Siemens acquired IndX software (realtime data organisation and presentation).<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The same year in an unrelated development Siemens reopened its office in <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Also in 2003 agreed to buy Alstom Industrial Turbines; a manufacturer of small, medium and industrial gas turbines for €1.1 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> On 11 February 2003, Siemens planned to shorten phones' shelf life by bringing out annual Xelibri lines, with new devices launched as spring -summer and autumn-winter collections.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> On 6 March 2003, the company opened an office in San Jose.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> On 7 March 2003, the company announced that it planned to gain 10 per cent of the mainland China market for handsets.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> On 18 March 2003, the company unveiled the latest in its series of Xelibri fashion phones.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2004, the wind energy company Bonus Energy in <a href="/wiki/Brande" title="Brande">Brande</a>, Denmark was acquired,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> forming <a href="/wiki/Siemens_Wind_Power" title="Siemens Wind Power">Siemens Wind Power</a> division.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Also in 2004 Siemens invested in Dasan Networks (South Korea, broadband network equipment) acquiring ~40% of the shares,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nokia_Siemens" class="mw-redirect" title="Nokia Siemens">Nokia Siemens</a> disinvested itself of the shares in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The same year Siemens acquired Photo-Scan (UK, <a href="/wiki/CCTV" class="mw-redirect" title="CCTV">CCTV</a> systems),<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> US Filter Corporation (water and Waste Water Treatment Technologies/ Solutions, acquired from <a href="/wiki/Veolia" title="Veolia">Veolia</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Hunstville Electronics Corporation (automobile electronics, acquired from <a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and Chantry Networks (<a href="/wiki/WLAN" class="mw-redirect" title="WLAN">WLAN</a> equipment).<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2005, Siemens sold the <a href="/wiki/Siemens_mobile" class="mw-redirect" title="Siemens mobile">Siemens mobile</a> manufacturing business to <a href="/wiki/BenQ" title="BenQ">BenQ</a>, forming the <a href="/wiki/BenQ-Siemens" title="BenQ-Siemens">BenQ-Siemens</a> division. Also in 2005 Siemens acquired Flender Holding GmbH (<a href="/wiki/Bocholt,_Germany" title="Bocholt, Germany">Bocholt</a>, Germany, gears/industrial drives),<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Bewator AB (building security systems),<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Wheelabrator Air Pollution Control, Inc. (Industrial and power station dust control systems),<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> AN Windenergie GmbH. (Wind energy),<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Power Technologies Inc. (<a href="/wiki/Schenectady" class="mw-redirect" title="Schenectady">Schenectady</a>, USA, energy industry software and training),<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> CTI Molecular Imaging (<a href="/wiki/Positron_emission_tomography" title="Positron emission tomography">Positron emission tomography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_imaging" title="Molecular imaging">molecular imaging</a> systems),<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Myrio (<a href="/wiki/IPTV" title="IPTV">IPTV</a> systems),<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Shaw Power Technologoes International Ltd (UK/USA, electrical engineering consulting, acquired from <a href="/wiki/Shaw_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Shaw Group">Shaw Group</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> and Transmitton (<a href="/wiki/Ashby_de_la_Zouch" class="mw-redirect" title="Ashby de la Zouch">Ashby de la Zouch</a> UK, rail and other industry control and asset management).<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2006, Siemens announced the purchase of Bayer Diagnostics, which was incorporated into the Medical Solutions Diagnostics division on 1 January 2007,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> also in 2006 Siemens acquired Controlotron (New York) (ultrasonic flow meters)<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Also in 2006 Siemens acquired Diagnostic Products Corp., Kadon Electro Mechanical Services Ltd. (now TurboCare Canada Ltd.), Kühnle, Kopp, &amp; Kausch AG, Opto Control, and VistaScape Security Systems<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wildorado_Wind_Ranch,_Oldham_County,_TX_IMG_4919.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Wildorado_Wind_Ranch%2C_Oldham_County%2C_TX_IMG_4919.JPG/220px-Wildorado_Wind_Ranch%2C_Oldham_County%2C_TX_IMG_4919.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Wildorado_Wind_Ranch%2C_Oldham_County%2C_TX_IMG_4919.JPG/330px-Wildorado_Wind_Ranch%2C_Oldham_County%2C_TX_IMG_4919.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Wildorado_Wind_Ranch%2C_Oldham_County%2C_TX_IMG_4919.JPG/440px-Wildorado_Wind_Ranch%2C_Oldham_County%2C_TX_IMG_4919.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wildorado_Wind_Ranch,_Oldham_County,_TX_IMG_4919.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Siemens power generating wind turbine towers</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In March 2007, a Siemens board member was temporarily arrested and accused of illegally financing a business-friendly labour association which competes against the union <a href="/wiki/IG_Metall" title="IG Metall">IG Metall</a>. He has been released on bail. Offices of the labour union and of Siemens have been searched. Siemens denies any wrongdoing.<sup id="cite_ref-ftcom_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ftcom-69">[69]</a></sup> In April the Fixed Networks, Mobile Networks and Carrier Services divisions of Siemens merged with <a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">Nokia</a>'s Network Business Group in a 50/50 joint venture, creating a fixed and mobile network company called <a href="/wiki/Nokia_Siemens_Networks" class="mw-redirect" title="Nokia Siemens Networks">Nokia Siemens Networks</a>. Nokia delayed the merger<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> due to bribery investigations against Siemens.<sup id="cite_ref-iht0_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iht0-71">[71]</a></sup> In October 2007, a court in Munich found that the company had bribed public officials in Libya, Russia, and Nigeria in return for the awarding of contracts; four former Nigerian Ministers of Communications were among those named as recipients of the payments. The company admitted to having paid the bribes and agreed to pay a fine of 201 million euros. In December 2007, the Nigerian government cancelled a contract with Siemens due to the bribery findings.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also in 2007, Siemens acquired Vai Ingdesi Automation (Argentina, Industrial Automation), <a href="/wiki/UGS_Corp." title="UGS Corp.">UGS Corp.</a>, Dade Behring, Sidelco (<a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, Canada), S/D Engineers Inc., and Gesellschaft für Systemforschung und Dienstleistungen im Gesundheitswesen mbH (GSD) (Germany).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2008, Siemens AG announced a joint venture of the <a href="/wiki/Siemens_Enterprise_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Siemens Enterprise Communications">Enterprise Communications</a> business with <a href="/wiki/The_Gores_Group" title="The Gores Group">the Gores Group</a>, renamed <a href="/wiki/Unify_GmbH_%26_Co._KG" class="mw-redirect" title="Unify GmbH &amp; Co. KG">Unify</a> in 2013. The Gores Group holding a majority interest of 51% stake, with Siemens AG holding a minority interest of 49%.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 2008, Siemens Project Ventures invested $15 million in the <a href="/wiki/Arava_Power_Company" title="Arava Power Company">Arava Power Company</a>. In a press release published that month, <a href="/wiki/Peter_L%C3%B6scher" title="Peter Löscher">Peter Löscher</a>, President and CEO of Siemens AG said: “This investment is another consequential step in further strengthening our green and sustainable technologies”. Siemens now holds a 40% stake in the company. <sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2009, Siemens announced to sell its 34% stake in Framatome, complaining limited managerial influence. In March, it announced to form an alliance with <a href="/wiki/Rosatom" title="Rosatom">Rosatom</a> of Russia to engage in nuclear-power activities.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ110415_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ110415-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2009, <a href="/wiki/Fujitsu_Siemens_Computers" title="Fujitsu Siemens Computers">Fujitsu Siemens Computers</a> became <a href="/wiki/Fujitsu_Technology_Solutions" title="Fujitsu Technology Solutions">Fujitsu Technology Solutions</a> as a result of Fujitsu buying out Siemens' share of the company.</p>
<p>In October 2009, Siemens signed a $418 million contract to buy <a href="/wiki/Solel" title="Solel">Solel Solar Systems</a> an Israeli company in the solar thermal power business.<sup id="cite_ref-Siemens_ERE200910.13e_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siemens_ERE200910.13e-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2010, Siemens agreed to sell its IT Solutions and Services subsidiary for €850 million to <a href="/wiki/Atos" title="Atos">Atos</a>. As part of the deal, Siemens agreed to take a 15% stake in the enlarged Atos, to be held for a minimum of five years. In addition, Siemens concluded a seven-year outsourcing contract worth around €5.5 billion, under which Atos will provide managed services and systems integration to Siemens.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2011_to_present">2011 to present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2011 to present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In March 2011, it was decided to list <a href="/wiki/Osram" title="Osram">Osram</a> on the stock market in the autumn, but CEO Peter Löscher said Siemens intended to retain a long-term interest in the company, which was already independent from the technological and managerial viewpoints.</p>
<p>In September 2011, Siemens, which had been responsible for constructing all 17 of Germany's existing nuclear power plants, announced that it would exit the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_sector" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear sector">nuclear sector</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima disaster</a> and the subsequent changes to German energy policy. Chief executive Peter Löscher has supported the German government's planned <i><a href="/wiki/Energiewende" class="mw-redirect" title="Energiewende">Energiewende</a></i>, its transition to renewable energy technologies, calling it a "project of the century" and saying Berlin's target of reaching 35% renewable energy sources by 2020 was feasible.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2012, Siemens acquired the Rail division of <a href="/wiki/Invensys" title="Invensys">Invensys</a> for £1.7 billion. In the same month, Siemens made the announcement of acquiring a <a href="/wiki/Privately_held_company" title="Privately held company">privately held company</a>, LMS International NV.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 2013, Nokia acquired 100% of the company Nokia Siemens Networks, with a buy-out of Siemens AG, ending Siemens role in telecommunication.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 2013, Siemens won a $966.8 million order for power plant components from oil firm <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Aramco" title="Saudi Aramco">Saudi Aramco</a>, the largest bid it has ever received from the Saudi company.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014, Siemens plans to build a $264 million facility for making offshore wind turbines in Paull, England, as Britain’s wind power rapidly expands. Siemens chose the Hull area on the east coast of England because it is close to other large offshore projects planned in coming years. The new plant is expected to begin producing turbine rotor blades in 2016. The plant and the associated service center, in <a href="/wiki/Green_Port_Hull" class="mw-redirect" title="Green Port Hull">Green Port Hull</a> nearby, will employ about 1,000 workers. The facilities will serve the UK market, where the electricity that major power producers generate from wind grew by about 38 percent in 2013, representing about 6 percent of total electricity, according to government figures. There are also plans to increase Britain’s wind-generating capacity at least threefold by 2020, to 14 gigawatts.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 2014, Rolls-Royce agreed to sell its gas turbine and compressor energy business to Siemens for £1 Billion.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In June 2014 Siemens and <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Heavy_Industries" title="Mitsubishi Heavy Industries">Mitsubishi Heavy Industries</a> announced their formation of joint ventures to bid for <a href="/wiki/Alstom" title="Alstom">Alstom</a>'s troubled energy and transportation businesses (in locomotives, steam turbines, and aircraft engines). A rival bid by <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> (GE) has been criticized by French government sources, who consider Alstom's operations as a "vital national interest" at a moment when the French unemployment level stands above 10% and some voters are turning towards the far-right.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 2014, Siemens said it would buy U.S. oilfield equipment maker <a href="/wiki/Dresser-Rand_Group" title="Dresser-Rand Group">Dresser-Rand Group</a> Inc for $7.6 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2016, Siemens announced the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Electronic_design_automation" title="Electronic design automation">EDA</a> company <a href="/wiki/Mentor_Graphics" title="Mentor Graphics">Mentor Graphics</a> for $4.5 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Products.2C_services_and_contribution">Products, services and contribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Products, services and contribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulu_Pandan_MRT_Depot,_Singapore_-_20050830.jpg" class="image"><img alt=" Siemens C651 trains for the Singapore MRT at Ulu Pandan Depot" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Ulu_Pandan_MRT_Depot%2C_Singapore_-_20050830.jpg/220px-Ulu_Pandan_MRT_Depot%2C_Singapore_-_20050830.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Ulu_Pandan_MRT_Depot%2C_Singapore_-_20050830.jpg/330px-Ulu_Pandan_MRT_Depot%2C_Singapore_-_20050830.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Ulu_Pandan_MRT_Depot%2C_Singapore_-_20050830.jpg/440px-Ulu_Pandan_MRT_Depot%2C_Singapore_-_20050830.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1152" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulu_Pandan_MRT_Depot,_Singapore_-_20050830.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Siemens_C651" title="Siemens C651">Siemens C651</a> trains for the <a href="/wiki/Mass_Rapid_Transit_(Singapore)" title="Mass Rapid Transit (Singapore)">Singapore MRT</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ulu_Pandan_Depot" title="Ulu Pandan Depot">Ulu Pandan Depot</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Siemens offers a wide range of electrical engineering- and electronics-related products and services.<sup id="cite_ref-reutprof_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutprof-87">[87]</a></sup> Its products can be broadly divided into the following categories: buildings-related products; drives, automation and industrial plant-related products; energy-related products; lighting; medical products; and transportation and logistics-related products.<sup id="cite_ref-reutprof_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutprof-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Siemens buildings-related products include building-automation equipment and systems; building-operations equipment and systems; building fire-safety equipment and systems; building-security equipment and systems; and low-voltage switchgear including circuit protection and distribution products.<sup id="cite_ref-reutprof_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutprof-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Siemens drives, automation and industrial plant-related products include motors and drives for conveyor belts; pumps and compressors; heavy duty motors and drives for rolling steel mills; compressors for oil and gas pipelines; mechanical components including gears for wind turbines and cement mills; automation equipment and systems and controls for production machinery and machine tools; and industrial plant for water processing and raw material processing.<sup id="cite_ref-reutprof_87-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutprof-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Siemens energy-related products include gas and steam turbines; generators; compressors; on- and offshore wind turbines; high-voltage transmission products; power transformers; high-voltage switching products and systems; alternating and direct current transmission systems; medium-voltage components and systems; and power automation products.<sup id="cite_ref-reutprof_87-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutprof-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Siemens OSRAM subsidiary produces lighting products including incandescent, halogen, compact fluorescent, fluorescent, high-intensity discharge and Xenon lamps; opto-electronic semiconductor light sources such as light emitting diodes (LEDs), organic LEDs, high power laser diodes, LED systems and LED luminaires; electronic equipment including electronic ballasts; lighting control and management systems; and related precision components.<sup id="cite_ref-reutprof_87-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutprof-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Siemens medical products include clinical information technology systems; hearing instruments; in-vitro diagnostics equipment; imaging equipment including angiography, computed tomography, fluoroscopy, magnetic resonance, mammography, molecular imaging ultrasound, and x-ray equipment; and radiation oncology and particle therapy equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-reutprof_87-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutprof-87">[87]</a></sup> As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Siemens finalized the sale of its hearing-aid (hearing instruments) business to Sivantos.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Siemens transportation and logistics-related products include equipment and systems for rail transportation including rail vehicles for mass transit, regional and long-distance transportation, locomotives, equipment and systems for rail electrification, central control systems, interlockings, and automated train controls; equipment and systems for road traffic including traffic detection, information and guidance; equipment and systems for airport logistics including cargo tracking and baggage handling; and equipment and systems for postal automation including letter parcel sorting.<sup id="cite_ref-reutprof_87-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutprof-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Siemens also completed a world record in 2012 for the most electricity generated by bicycles in an hour. Generating 4,630 watts in an hour in Melbourne, Australia, on December 11, 2012<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operations">Operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Siemens is incorporated in Germany and has its corporate headquarters in Munich.<sup id="cite_ref-annrep2011_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annrep2011-91">[91]</a></sup> It has operations in around 190 countries and approximately 285 production and manufacturing facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-annrep2011_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annrep2011-91">[91]</a></sup> Siemens had around 360,000 employees as of 30 September 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-annrep2011_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annrep2011-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Electrification, automation and digitalization are the long-term growth fields of Siemens. In order to take full advantage of the market potential in these fields, Siemens businesses are bundled into nine divisions and healthcare as a separately managed business.</p>
<ul>
<li>Power and Gas</li>
<li>Wind Power and Renewables</li>
<li>Power Generation Services</li>
<li>Energy Management</li>
<li>Building Technologies</li>
<li>Mobility</li>
<li>Digital Factory</li>
<li>Process Industries and Drives</li>
<li>Financial Services</li>
<li>Healthineers</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_and_development">Research and development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Research and development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2011, Siemens invested a total of €3.925 billion in research and development, equivalent to 5.3% of revenues.<sup id="cite_ref-annrep2011_91-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annrep2011-91">[91]</a></sup> As of 30 September 2011, Siemens had approximately 11,800 Germany-based employees engaged in research and development and approximately 16,000 in the rest of the world, of whom the majority were based in one of Austria, China, Croatia, Denmark, France, India, Mexico, Slovakia, Sweden, Switzerland, the United Kingdom and the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-annrep2011_91-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annrep2011-91">[91]</a></sup> As of 30 September 2011, Siemens held approximately 53,300 patents worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-annrep2011_91-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annrep2011-91">[91]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Joint_ventures">Joint ventures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Joint ventures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Siemens' current joint ventures include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Siemens Traction Equipment Ltd. (STEZ), <a href="/wiki/Zhuzhou" title="Zhuzhou">Zhuzhou</a> China, is a joint venture between Siemens, <a href="/wiki/Zhuzhou_CSR_Times_Electric" class="mw-redirect" title="Zhuzhou CSR Times Electric">Zhuzhou CSR Times Electric Co., Ltd.</a> (TEC) and <a href="/wiki/Zhuzhou_Electric_Locomotive_Co.,_Ltd." class="mw-redirect" title="Zhuzhou Electric Locomotive Co., Ltd.">CSR Zhuzhou Electric Locomotive Co., Ltd.</a> (ZELC). which produces AC drive electric locomotives and AC locomotive traction components.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Silcar was a joint venture between Siemens Ltd and Thiess Services Pty Ltd until 2013. Silcar is a 3,000 person Australian organisation providing productivity and reliability for large scale and technically complex plant assets. Services include asset management, design, construction, operations and maintenance. Silcar operates across a range of industries and essential services including power generation, electrical distribution, manufacturing, mining and telecommunications. In July 2013, Thiess took full control.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>OMNETRIC Group, A Siemens &amp; Accenture Company</li>
</ul>
<p>In 2014, Siemens and Accenture formed a joint venture company called <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.OMNETRICGroup.com">OMNETRIC Group</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shareholders">Shareholders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Shareholders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The company has issued 881,000,000 shares of common stock. The largest single shareholder continues to be the founding shareholder, the <a href="/wiki/Siemens_family" title="Siemens family">Siemens family</a>, with a stake of 6.9%. 62% are held by institutional <a href="/wiki/Asset_manager" class="mw-redirect" title="Asset manager">asset managers</a>, the largest being two divisions of the world's largest asset manager <a href="/wiki/BlackRock" title="BlackRock">BlackRock</a>. 83.97% of the shares are considered <a href="/wiki/Public_float" title="Public float">public float</a>, however including such strategic investors as the State of <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a> (DIC Company Ltd.) with 3,04%, the <a href="/wiki/Government_Pension_Fund_of_Norway" title="Government Pension Fund of Norway">Government Pension Fund of Norway</a> with 2,5% and the Siemens AG itself with 3,04%. 19% are held by private investors, 13% by investors that are considered unidentifiable. 26% are owned by German investors, 21% by US investors, followed by the UK (11%), France (8%), Switzerland (8%) and a number of others (26%).<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Senior_management">Senior management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Senior management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Argentina">Argentina</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Argentina">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2014 a former Siemens executive Andres Truppel pleaded guilty to funneling nearly $100 million in bribes to Argentine government officials to win the ID card project for Siemens. Two months later Siemens agreed to pay a $1.6 billion penalty to US and German authorities to resolve the case.<sup id="cite_ref-timesofisrael.com_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timesofisrael.com-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: European Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In January 2007, Siemens was fined €396 million by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> for <a href="/wiki/Price_fixing" title="Price fixing">price fixing</a> in EU electricity markets through a <a href="/wiki/Cartel" title="Cartel">cartel</a> involving 11 companies, including <a href="/wiki/ABB_Asea_Brown_Boveri" class="mw-redirect" title="ABB Asea Brown Boveri">ABB</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alstom" title="Alstom">Alstom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fuji_Electric" title="Fuji Electric">Fuji Electric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hitachi_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="Hitachi Japan">Hitachi Japan</a>, AE Power Systems, <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Electric_Corp" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitsubishi Electric Corp">Mitsubishi Electric Corp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schneider_Electric" title="Schneider Electric">Schneider</a>, <a href="/wiki/Areva" title="Areva">Areva</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toshiba" title="Toshiba">Toshiba</a> and <a href="/wiki/VA_Tech_Wabag" title="VA Tech Wabag">VA Tech</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fine_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fine-101">[101]</a></sup> According to the Commission, "between 1988 and 2004, the companies rigged bids for procurement contracts, fixed prices, allocated projects to each other, shared markets and exchanged commercially important and confidential information."<sup id="cite_ref-fine_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fine-101">[101]</a></sup> Siemens was given the highest fine of €396 million, more than half of the total, for its alleged leadership role in the activity.</p>
<p>Siemens agreed to pay a record $1.34 billion in fines in December 2008<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> after being investigated for bribery. The investigation found questionable payments of roughly €1.3 billion, from 2002 to 2006 that triggered a broad range of inquiries in Germany, the United States and other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> In May 2007 a German court convicted two former executives of paying about €6 million in bribes from 1999 to 2002 to help Siemens win <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">turbine</a> supply contracts with <a href="/wiki/Enel" title="Enel">Enel</a>, an Italian energy company. The contracts were valued at about €450 million. Siemens was fined €38 million.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran">Iran</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Iran">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nokia_Siemens" class="mw-redirect" title="Nokia Siemens">Nokia Siemens</a> supplied telecommunications equipment to the Iranian telecom company that included the ability to intercept and monitor telecommunications, a facility known as "<a href="/wiki/Lawful_intercept" class="mw-redirect" title="Lawful intercept">lawful intercept</a>". The equipment was believed to have been used in the suppression of the <a href="/wiki/2009_Iranian_election_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2009 Iranian election protests">2009 Iranian election protests</a>, leading to criticism of the company, including by the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a>. Nokia-Siemens later divested its call monitoring business, and reduced its activities in Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Siemens has been <a href="/wiki/Siemens_Greek_bribery_scandal" title="Siemens Greek bribery scandal">accused of bribing Greek officials</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> In 2008, it was revealed that Siemens had bribed the two main political parties of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> for approximately 10 years to be the sole provider of mechanical and electrical equipment of the Greek state.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> After the exposure the German authorities moved to arrest the representatives of Siemens in Greece, who had managed to escape from the Greek authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The German judicial system didn't allow the Greek authorities to cross-question the representatives.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> As a result, there wasn't any solid evidence against the corrupt politicians, who weren't arrested and continue to be active in the Greek political system.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Meanwhile, the Greek state cancelled the planned business deals.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In August 2012, the Greek government signed a settlement with Siemens for these corruption cases worth 330 million euros.<sup id="cite_ref-telfin20122_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-telfin20122-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>The trial of the persons accused of involvement in the scandal began on 24 February 2017. A total of 64 individuals are accused, both Greek and German nationals.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The central figure of the scandal however, ex-Siemens chief executive in Greece Michael Christoforakos, against whom European arrest warrants are pending<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> will likely be absent, as Germany refuses his extradition to this day. Initially arrested in Germany in 2009, the accusations against him by German courts have been dropped, and he since lives free in this country.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Greece has been demanding his extradition since 2009, and considers him a fugitive from justice.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1914 the <a href="/wiki/Siemens_scandal" title="Siemens scandal">Siemens scandal</a> erupted in Japan over bribes by paid by both Siemens and <a href="/w/index.php?title=VIckers&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="VIckers (page does not exist)">VIckers</a> to Japanese naval authorities to win shipbuilding contracts.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Israel">Israel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Israel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2014 Israeli prosecutors decreed that Siemens should pay US$42.7 million penalty and appoint an external inspector to supervise its business in Israel in exchange for state prosecutors dropping charges of securities fraud. According to the indictment, "Siemens systematically paid bribes to Israel Electric Corporation executives so they would utilize their positions in order to favor and advance the interests of Siemens".<sup id="cite_ref-timesofisrael.com_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timesofisrael.com-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nigeria">Nigeria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Nigeria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Siemens has been found guilty in a bribery scandal in Nigeria too, and fined €201 million by a Munich court in 2007,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> and N7 billion to the Nigerian government in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siemens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Weiher, Siegfried von /Herbert Goetzeler (1984). <i>The Siemens Company, Its Historical Role in the Progress of Electrical Engineering 1847–1980</i>, 2nd ed. Berlin and Munich.</li>
<li>Feldenkirchen, Wilfried (2000). <i>Siemens, From Workshop to Global Player</i>, Munich.</li>
<li>Feldenkirchen, Wilfried / Eberhard Posner (2005): <i>The Siemens Entrepreneurs</i>, Continuity and Change, 1847–2005, Ten Portraits, Munich.</li>
<li>Greider, William (1997). <i>One World, Ready or Not</i>. Penguin Press. <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0713992115" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 0-7139-9211-5</a>.</li>
<li>Margarete Buber: 303f As prisoners of Stalin and Hitler, Frankf / Main, Berlin 1993</li>
<li>See Carola Sachse: Jewish forced labor and non-Jewish women and men at Siemens from 1940 to 1945, in: International Scientific Correspondence, No. 1/1991, pp.&#160;12–24; Karl-Heinz Roth: forced labor in the Siemens Group (1938 -1945). Facts, controversies, problems, in: Hermann Kaienburg (ed.): concentration camps and the German Economy 1939–1945 (Social studies, H. 34), Opladen 1996, pp.&#160;149–168; Wilfried Feldenkirchen: 1918–1945 Siemens, Munich 1995, Ulrike fire, Claus Füllberg-Stolberg, Sylvia Kempe: work at Ravensbrück concentration camp, in: Women in concentration camps. Bergen-Belsen. Ravensbrück, Bremen, 1994, pp.&#160;55–69; Ursula Krause-Schmitt: The path to the Siemens stock led past the crematorium, in: Information. German Resistance Study Group, Frankfurt / Main, 18 Jg, No. 37/38, Nov. 1993, pp.&#160;38–46; Sigrid Jacobeit: working at Siemens in Ravensbrück, in: Dietrich Eichholz (eds) War and economy. Studies on German economic history 1939–1945, Berlin 1999.</li>
<li>Bundesarchiv Berlin, NS 19, No. 968, Communication on the creation of the barracks for the Siemens &amp; Halske, the planned production and the planned expansion for 2,500 prisoners "after direct discussions with this company": Economic and Administrative Main Office of the SS ( WVHA), Oswald Pohl, secretly, to Reichsführer SS (RFSS), Heinrich Himmler, dated 20 October 1942.</li>
<li>Karl-Heinz Roth: forced labor in the Siemens Group, with a summary table, page 157 See also Ursula Krause-Schmitt: "The road to Siemens stock led to the crematorium past over," pp.&#160;36f, where, according to the catalogs of the International Tracing Service Arolsen and Martin Weinmann (eds.).. The Nazi camp system, Frankfurt / Main 1990 and Feldkirchen: Siemens 1918–1945, pp.&#160;198–214, and in particular the associated annotations 91–187.</li>
<li>MSS in the estate include Wanda Kiedrzy'nska, in: National Library of Poland, Warsaw, Manuscript Division, Sygn. akc 12013/1 and archive the memorial I/6-7-139 RA: see also: Woman Ravensbruck concentration camp. An overall presentation, State Justice Administration in Ludwigsburg, IV ART 409-Z 39/59, April 1972, pp.&#160;129ff.</li>
</ul>
